   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d62086579" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>MXene nanosheets and ordered porous carbons both have their own advantages and disadvantages. Assembling and combining the advantages of the two will be a good choice for battery electrode hosts of active materials. In this work, an electrostatic separation-adsorption strategy is proposed to realize the ordered alternating self-assembly of MXene nanosheets and ordered porous carbon (MPOC), obtaining a unique wall-like porous material with a high conductivity and interconnected porous nanostructure, which strengthens the transfer rate of electrons and ions simultaneously. Meanwhile, the introduction of N-doping from porous carbon into MPOC prolongs the cycle life. When use red phosphorus (RP) as active materials, the MPOC@RP anode exhibited high-capacity output (2454.3 and 2408.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) at 0.1 C) and long cycle life (the decay rates per cycle of 0.028% and 0.036% after 1500 and 1200 cycles at 2 C in LIBs and SIBs respectively). The successful application in RP anodes displays great potential in other electrode materials such as silicon, sulfur, selenium, and so on. Meanwhile, this strategy is also effective to design other composites materials like MXene and carbon nanotubes, MXene and Graphene, and so on.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202309702-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202309702-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Red phosphorus (RP) is one of the most promising anode candidates for the next-generation lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to the high theoretical specific capacity (2596 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), feasible reaction potential (≈0.7&nbsp;V vs Li<sup>+</sup>/Li and ≈0.3&nbsp;V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) and natural abundance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0001_R_d62086571e356" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater.">1</a>, <a href="#smll202309702-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0002_R_d62086571e356" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the formidable volume expansion and poor electrical conductivity of RP often cause severe electrode pulverization and sluggish electrochemical reaction kinetics, resulting in battery performance deterioration.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0003_R_d62086571e362" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Energy Lett.">3</a>, <a href="#smll202309702-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0004_R_d62086571e362" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Stor. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the solvable behavior of polyphosphides leads to active materials loss and a series of parasitic reactions, impairing the rate capability and cycle life.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0005_R_d62086571e367" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Stor. Mater.">5</a>, <a href="#smll202309702-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0006_R_d62086571e367" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nano Lett.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To target the issues, working with high-conductivity architectures is one of the most popular tactics to improve the performance of the RP anode.</p>
         
         <p>As a congregation of transition metal carbides, nitrides, and carbonitrides, MXene has a typical 2D structure, high conductivity comparable to metals, and extremely lightweight.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0007_R_d62086571e375" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Rev. Chem.">7</a>, <a href="#smll202309702-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0008_R_d62086571e375" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Electron. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the field of energy storage, MXene is often used directly as an electrode material<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0009_R_d62086571e380" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Energy Lett.">9</a>, <a href="#smll202309702-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0010_R_d62086571e380" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Nano">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> or as a conductive host for LIBs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0011_R_d62086571e385" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> SIBs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0012_R_d62086571e391" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> magnesium-ion batteries (MIBs),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0013_R_d62086571e396" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Small">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> zinc-ion batteries (ZIBs),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0014_R_d62086571e402" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">14</a>, <a href="#smll202309702-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0015_R_d62086571e402" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Nano">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and supercapacitors.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0016_R_d62086571e407" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence, MXene has a wide application potential in improving the electrochemical performance of red phosphorus.</p>
         
         <p>However, MXene nanosheets prepared by chemical etching are prone to stacking, which significantly reduces the storage space and buffer space when used as a host for electrode materials with severe volume expansion effects such as sulfur, selenium, silicon, germanium, red phosphorus, etc. In order to solve the above problems, a common way for researchers is to make layered MXene nanosheets into MXene nano-hollow spheres<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0017_R_d62086571e415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Mater. Interfaces">17</a>, <a href="#smll202309702-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0018_R_d62086571e415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Stor. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> or increase the interlayer distance by ion intercalation to create storage space, but because the ion radius is small, the obtained space is limited. Therefore, if inserting porous materials between nanosheet layers, it not only solves the stacking problem of MXene nanosheets but also increases the specific surface area, thereby increasing the reactive active sites and storage space.</p>
         
         <p>Porous carbon materials are widely used in energy storage devices such as lithium batteries, sodium batteries, and supercapacitors due to their excellent electrical conductivity, stable chemical properties, large specific surface area, and low preparation cost.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0019_R_d62086571e423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, 3D ordered porous carbon has a unique bicontinuous transport structure of electrons and ions, a uniform pore size, and an orderly distribution of pores. The continuous framework and interconnected macropores shorten the transport distances of electrons and ions, respectively, facilitating rapid charge transport.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0020_R_d62086571e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater.">20</a>, <a href="#smll202309702-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0021_R_d62086571e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Regular and orderly macropores precisely control the specific surface area of porous carbon, facilitate the study of the impact of porous carbon structure on device performance, and promote the uniform distribution of active materials, electrons, and ions to achieve uniform electrochemical reactions, which inhibits the local overheating inside the electrodes to reduce battery hazards.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0022_R_d62086571e433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Nano">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In this work, we precisely assembled MXene nanosheets and ordered porous carbon in an ordered alternating way, and a unique wall-like porous composite materials (denoted as MPOC) were prepared for the first time. The discrete advantages of the two were combined closely and utilized completely. The highly conductive MXene nanosheets promote the rapid transport of electrons, and the ordered porous carbon with high specific surface area provides sufficient storage space for active materials and abundant reactive sites. The introduction of N-doping into the composite materials greatly inhibits the dissolution and the loss of electrochemical reaction intermediates. In order to verify the advantages of wall-like porous materials, electrochemical performance of red phosphorus as the active material and wall-like porous materials as the host was characterized. As a result, MPOC@RP anode exhibited a high specific capacity of 2454.3 and 2408.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in LIBs and SIBs at 0.1 C and an impressive cycle life of &gt;1500 and 1200 cycles at 2 C in LIBs and SIBs with the decay rates per cycle of 0.028% and 0.036% respectively. This kind of wall-like porous material not only effectively solves the problems of stacking of 2D nanosheets, but also provides a universal design strategy for other composites materials like MXene and carbon nanotubes, MXene and Graphene, and so on.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202309702-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202309702-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202309702-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202309702-sec-0030-title">2.1 Material Characterization</h3>
            
            <p><b>Scheme</b> <a href="#smll202309702-fig-0007">1</a> shows a schematic diagram of the synthesis of wall-like porous materials. First, the ZIF-8 precursor solution was infiltrated into the silica colloidal opal template (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a>, Supporting Information) by vacuum pressure and grown in situ in the interstices of the opal template (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b</a>, Supporting Information). Subsequent high-temperature carbonization (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1c</a>, Supporting Information) and removal of silica colloidal microspheres yielded ordered porous carbon (OC) (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0001">1a</a>,<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0001">b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1d</a>, Supporting Information). It clearly shows that OC has a periodic macroporous structure with a pore diameter of 340&nbsp;nm. The macropores are in a close-packed hexagonal arrangement and connected to each other through three uniform mesopores. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0001">1c</a> shows that C, N, and O elements were evenly distributed on the entire skeleton, demonstrating that N were successfully doped into the carbon matrix. The N content in the carbon matrix was 17.16 wt.%. High content of N doping improves the electrical conductivity and reactivity of the carbon matrix.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202309702-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f01769f0-f198-4a39-a3f3-8dff09b38e8a/smll202309702-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f01769f0-f198-4a39-a3f3-8dff09b38e8a/smll202309702-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5166b178-63b5-4c80-87b9-b8ddf3aff2b3/smll202309702-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f01769f0-f198-4a39-a3f3-8dff09b38e8a/smll202309702-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202309702-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202309702" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of the fabrication of MPOC@RP composite.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202309702-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3c47f3c1-f229-4f59-ab3b-42aa9210f055/smll202309702-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/3c47f3c1-f229-4f59-ab3b-42aa9210f055/smll202309702-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/eb8910aa-7635-47fe-bcbd-9bd8807a00bf/smll202309702-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3c47f3c1-f229-4f59-ab3b-42aa9210f055/smll202309702-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202309702-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202309702" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) SEM image, b) TEM image, and c) HAADF-STEM image and corresponding elemental mappings of OC. d) SEM image, e) TEM image, and f) AFM topography and corresponding thickness profile of the MXene. g) SEM images of MPOC hybrid. h,i) SEM image and corresponding elemental mappings of MPOC@RP composite.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>By selectively etching away the Al interphase of MAX-Ti<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a</a>, Supporting Information), multilayered and stacked Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2b</a>, Supporting Information) was obtained. Single-layer or few-layer MXene nanosheets were obtained by a followed ultrasonic stripping. Since the surface of MXene nanosheets was covered by abundant negatively charged groups such as ─OH, ─F, or ─O,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0023_R_d62086571e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the zeta potential showed a negative charge (−40.7&nbsp;mV) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Many surface functional groups make it hydrophilic and uniformly dispersed in water, exhibiting an obvious Tyndall effect<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0024_R_d62086571e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ACS Energy Lett.">24</a>, <a href="#smll202309702-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0025_R_d62086571e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Small">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). The thickness of the MXene nanosheets was ultra-thin, and it presented a wrinkled and self-stacked sheet shape (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0001">1d,e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2c,d</a>, Supporting Information). According to the atomic force microscope (AFM) image results, the single layer thickness of the MXene sheet was ≈1.64&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0001">1f</a>).</p>
            
            <p>Then, Poly(diallyldimethylammonium chloride) (PDDA)&nbsp;was used to modify OC to make its surface positively charged. The zeta potential revealed a charge of 26.7&nbsp;mV (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Subsequently, under the adsorption of opposite charge, OC and MXene nanosheets orderly self-assembled, forming a unique wall-like porous structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0001">1g</a>). The dispersed OC units were evenly drawn on both sides of the MXene sheet, realizing the regular and dense connection between the OC units. Compared with pure OC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1d</a>, Supporting Information), MPOC significantly improves the structural order and electron transport continuity. As a result, the conductivity of MPOC was three times higher than OC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). At the same time, the stacking phenomenon of MXene nanosheets also disappeared. For comparison, we directly mixed OC with MXene nanosheets (denoted as MOC). The exfoliated MXene nanosheets would re-stack and deposit in the form of multilayered bulk on the OC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). MXene nanosheets and OC both still existed in separate forms, indicating that PDDA modification is helpful for the regular assembly of MXene nanosheets and OC units.</p>
            
            <p>The X-ray diffraction (XRD) comparison results of MPOC, OC, and MXene are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information). In MXene, the characteristic peak at 7.1° corresponded to the (002) crystal plane of a typical Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> phase with an interlayer spacing of 12.4 Å. In addition to this obvious strong peak, four weak peaks at 14.7°, 84.4°, 35.5°, and 41.7° were observed, corresponding to (004), (008), (0010), and (0012) crystal plane of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> phase, respectively, which was consistent with the literature reports.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0026_R_d62086571e607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Small">26</a>, <a href="#smll202309702-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0027_R_d62086571e607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In OC, only one distinct broad peak was detected at 26°, corresponding to the (002) crystal plane of amorphous carbon formed by carbonization of MOFs. When OC and MXene were self-assembled, the characteristic peaks of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> crystal phase and porous carbon coexisted in the composite material.</p>
            
            <p><b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0002">2a</a> shows the Raman results of MXene, OC, and MPOC. The two peaks located near the positions of 410.6 and 609.1 cm<sup>−1</sup> in MXene corresponding to the Ti-C bond vibration.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0023_R_d62086571e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#smll202309702-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0028_R_d62086571e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Energy Environ. Sci.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In OC, the two peaks appearing at ≈1367.4 and 1593.1&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> belonged to two typical characteristic bands of carbon, namely the D band induced by disorder or defects and the graphitized G band. The characteristic peaks of MXene and carbon simultaneously appeared in MPOC indicating that OC was successfully combined with MXene nanosheets. In order to investigate the specific surface area, pore size distribution, pore volume of the samples, N<sub>2</sub> adsorption-desorption analysis was carried out. A typical type IV hysteresis loop curve was observed in the high-pressure range (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0002">2b</a>), indicating that micropores and mesopores exist simultaneously in the porous structure of MPOC and OC.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0029_R_d62086571e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Small">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The corresponding pore size distribution curve further proves that the samples are rich in micro-mesoporous (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0002">2c</a>). The easily stackable MXene sheets exhibited a poor specific surface area of 5.89 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> and a volume of 0.025 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). When MXene was grafted with OC, the specific surface area of MPOC increased to 796.4 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, and the corresponding pore volume was 12.51 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>. The enlarged specific surface area and pore volume not only increases the mass loading of active materials, but also provides more pathways for ion transport and active sites for electrochemical reaction.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202309702-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2fcd753b-488f-404d-971f-d0acb0f353ab/smll202309702-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/2fcd753b-488f-404d-971f-d0acb0f353ab/smll202309702-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/164d9c6d-96cb-4f36-9859-b3afebfeed6c/smll202309702-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2fcd753b-488f-404d-971f-d0acb0f353ab/smll202309702-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202309702-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202309702" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Raman spectra of as-prepared samples, b) Curves of Nitrogen adsorption and desorption of as-prepared samples, c) Curves of pore-size distribution of as-prepared samples, d–f) Fitted curves of Ti 2p, C 1s, and N 1s XPS spectra of MPOC.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In order to gain insight into the elemental chemical bond states of samples, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was performed. In the full XPS spectrum shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a</a> (Supporting Information), the MXene nanosheets were mainly composed of Ti, C, O and F elements. When OC and MXene nanosheets were assembled, a N 1s peak at 399.5&nbsp;eV appeared in the spectrum of MPOC. Figures&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0002">2d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8b</a> (Supporting Information) are high-resolution Ti 2p XPS spectra of MPOC and MXene, fitted with three doublet peaks (Ti 2p3/2 and Ti 2p1/2).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0030_R_d62086571e702" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Energy Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The characteristic peaks at 455.6/461.5&nbsp;eV, 456.7/462.5&nbsp;eV, and 458.3/463.5&nbsp;eV correspond to the Ti─C, Ti(II) and Ti─O bonds of MXene,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0031_R_d62086571e708" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Mater.">31</a>-<a href="#smll202309702-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0033_R_d62086571e708" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Energy Stor. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively. The C 1s XPS spectrum of MXene nanosheets was fitted into four characteristic peaks at 281.4, 283.7, 284.2, and 288.9&nbsp;eV, corresponding to Ti─C, C─C, C─O, and O═C─O bonds<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0030_R_d62086571e713" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Energy Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a</a>, Supporting Information). Three peaks at 284.7, 285.7, and 288.4&nbsp;eV were fitted in OC, corresponding to C─C, C─N, and O═C─O bonds,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0034_R_d62086571e722" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Energy Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b</a>, Supporting Information). Among them, the characteristic peak at 284.7&nbsp;eV appeared as the strongest peak, indicating that graphitic carbon is the main component of this MOF-derived porous carbon. The C 1s in MPOC combined the fitting peaks of the above two substances, and Ti─C, C─C, C─O, C─N, and O═C─O bonds appeared at the same time (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0002">2e</a>). In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a</a> (Supporting Information), there was no signal peak of N in the pure MXene sheet. N 1s XPS spectrum in OC fitted four peaks at 398.4, 400.2, 402.2, and 406.5&nbsp;eV, which are pyridine N (N-6), pyrrole N (N-5), graphite N (N-Q) and N-O, indicating that N atoms are indeed doped into the carbon lattice<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0035_R_d62086571e736" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Energy Environ. Sci.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>(Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10b</a>, Supporting Information). Compared with other types of N, pyridine-N is more active because it effectively induces sufficient defects and more active sites, which is favorable for the adsorption of polyphosphides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0036_R_d62086571e745" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Mater.">36</a>, <a href="#smll202309702-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0037_R_d62086571e745" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Energy Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thanks to the abundant N element in the OC ligand, the MPOC composite not only has a novel structure, but also inherits a high N doping content (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0002">2f</a>). The introduction of N will greatly improve the conductivity of the overall electrode and increase the active sites for Li/Na ions storage. In the high-resolution O 1s XPS spectrum (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a</a>, Supporting Information), two strong peaks at 529.3 and 531.7&nbsp;eV in MXene corresponded to the Ti─O and Ti─OH bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0038_R_d62086571e757" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Energy Stor. Mater.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The O 1s XPS spectrum of OC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11b</a>) was fitted into three peaks at 531.4, 532.6, and 534.9&nbsp;eV, corresponding to C═O, C─OH, and N─O bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0039_R_d62086571e765" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Energy Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, the O 1s in MPOC possessed Ti─O, Ti─OH, C═O, C─OH ,and N─O bonds appeared simultaneously (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11c</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>To investigate the structural properties of MPOC, RP was used as the active material for battery performance characterization. Through the vaporization-condensation method, RP was successfully deposited in the pores of MPOC (MPOC@RP). For comparison, MXene@RP, OC@RP, and MOC@RP were synthesized in the same way. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0001">1h</a> is the SEM image of the MPOC loaded with RP. It was clearly seen that the basic skeleton shape of the carrier remained unchanged, but the macropores of OC were covered by active materials. No obvious aggregated RP particles were found, indicating that active materials were successfully infiltrated in the inner pores of MPOC. EDS results showed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0001">1i</a>) that Ti, C, N, and P elements were uniformly distributed throughout the MPOC@RP composite. The intensity of the characteristic signal peak of RP in the MPOC@RP composite was obviously weaker than that of the pure RP phase (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a</a>, Supporting Information). In the optical microscope images of the MXene@RP composite, substances different from the host color appeared (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12c,d</a>, Supporting Information), while the color of the MPOC@RP composite hardly changed (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12e,f</a>, Supporting Information). The hysteresis line in the isotherm curve of MPOC@RP disappears, and the distribution area of micro-mesopore size also decreased sharply (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0002">2b,c</a>). The above results all indicate that the composite of RP and MPOC has been successfully prepared. The XPS result of RP in the composite is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information). According to the fitting results, it involves two types of bonding of P─O and P─P. The P─O bonding primarily arises from the inevitable partial oxidation of red phosphorus, while the majority of P─P bonding indicates that red phosphorus exists in its elemental form in the composite material. In the TGA (Thermogravimetric Analysis) curves of the samples, as the temperature gradually increases, there is only one main sharp decline, indicating the stability of OC and MXene at high temperatures without decomposition or mass loss (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, from the curve shapes, it can be observed that red phosphorus begins to vaporize at ≈400&nbsp;°C, and at ≈450&nbsp;°C red phosphorus is completely expelled from the sample. The content of red phosphorus in the MPOC@RP composite accounted for 40.6%, which was significantly higher than that in MXene@RP, indicating that the introduction of OC increased the active material storage.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202309702-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202309702-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The lithiation/delithiation behavior of the MPOC@RP electrode was studied by cyclic voltammetry (CV) at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> in the range of 0.01-3&nbsp;V, as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0003">3a</a>. In the first CV curve, two reduction peaks were observed at 0.36 and 0.51, corresponding to the continuous lithiation process of RP (Li+P→Li<sub>x</sub>P, x = 1–3),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0040_R_d62086571e820" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Mater. Chem. A">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> accompanied with the generation of soluble lithium polyphosphide. The two oxidation peaks at 1.06 and 1.30 corresponded to the delithiation process of Li<sub>x</sub>P compounds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0041_R_d62086571e828" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ACS Nano">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the subsequent four cycles, the oxidation and reduction peaks were mainly concentrated at 1.26 and 0.67&nbsp;V, indicating that the MPOC@RP anode has a strong reaction reversibility.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202309702-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a0bc99c3-c94b-4058-92fa-bfd7aebaaa1d/smll202309702-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a0bc99c3-c94b-4058-92fa-bfd7aebaaa1d/smll202309702-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/940ca1c6-7a90-4930-813d-f10e11cb43ec/smll202309702-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a0bc99c3-c94b-4058-92fa-bfd7aebaaa1d/smll202309702-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202309702-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202309702" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV profiles of the MPOC@RP anode at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. Initial ten discharge/charge curves of b) the MPOC@RP anode and c) OC@RP anode between 0.01 and 3.0&nbsp;V at 0.1 C. d) Rate capacities of MPOC@RP anode in comparison to OC@RP and MXene@RP anodes. e) Comparison of the rate capacity between this work and other previously reported RP-based LIB anodes. f) Long-term cycling stability of the three anodes at 2 C. g) Cycling performance of the MPOC@RP anode at 10 C. h) Rate capacities of the MPOC@RP, OC@RP, and MXene@RP anodes for SIBs. i) Cycling performance of the three anodes at 2 C in SIBs. Note: a–g) LIBs; h,i) SIBs.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0003">3b</a> shows the first ten charge–discharge curves of the MPOC@RP anode. The voltage plateaus of charge–discharge were consistent with the CV results. At a current density of 0.1 C (1 C = 2600&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>), the MPOC@RP anode exhibited a high initial discharge/charge capacity of 3194.6/2410.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (calculated based on the mass of red phosphorus) and an initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 75.4%. The low ICE is attributed to the formation of a large-area solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer, leading to the irreversible consumption of electrolyte and metallic Li. After the third cycle, the reversible specific capacity of the MPOC@RP anode was stable at 2454.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which was close to the theoretical specific capacity of RP (2600 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), and the Coulombic efficiency exceeded 97%. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0003">3c</a> is the galvanostatic charge and discharge curve of the OC@RP anode at 0.1 C. During cycling, the polarization voltage (ΔEp) of the discharge/charge curve of the OC@RP anode increased gradually and reached 510&nbsp;mV at the 10th cycle. In contrast, the polarization voltage (ΔEp = 390&nbsp;mV) of the MPOC@RP anode was much lower than that of the OC@RP anode, indicating that the introduction of highly conductive MXene sheets in the MPOC@RP anode increases the lithiation/delithiation reaction kinetics, weakening the polarization of the electrode. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0003">3d</a>, the stable reversible specific capacities of the MPOC@RP anode at 0.3, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10 C are 2285.7, 2079.2, 1922.9, 1765.8, 1410.3, and 1055.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, higher than the corresponding specific capacities of OC@RP and MXene@RP at the same current. With the gradual increase of current density, the difference in specific capacity between the three became more obvious. Especially at a large rate of 10 C, the capacity of the MXene@RP anode was as low as 34.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which was almost negligible. When the current returned to 0.3 C, the reversible capacity of the MPOC@RP anode immediately returned to 2215.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating that the electrode still has excellent reversibility after high-rate charge and discharge. Even the specific capacity values were calculated based on the total mass of composite materials, MPOC@RP still exhibited a much higher capacities than traditional graphite anode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). Its excellent rate performance is mainly attributed to the fact that the interconnected porous channels in MPOC facilitate electrolyte penetration during cycling and increase the effective contact area between electrodes and electrolytes, while the highly conductive MXene bridges accelerate electron transport. The rate performance of the MPOC@RP anode at high current density also exceeds that of most phosphorus-based anodes reported in the literature,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0029_R_d62086571e886" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Small">29</a>, <a href="#smll202309702-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0042_R_d62086571e886" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - ACS Nano">42</a>-<a href="#smll202309702-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0047_R_d62086571e886" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - J. Mater. Chem. A">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0003">3e</a>.</p>
            
            <p>The long-term cycle performance of MPOC@RP, OC@RP, and MXene@RP anodes at a high rate of 2 C is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0003">3f</a>. After 1500 cycles, MPOC@RP exhibited a high specific capacity of 986.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity decay rate of 0.028% per cycle and a Coulombic efficiency close to 100%. Under the same cycle conditions, the specific capacity and decay rate of OC@RP anode were 483.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 0.042%, respectively, which was far lower than the cycle performance of MPOC@RP anode. In sharp contrast, the initial discharge capacity of the MXene@RP anode was 460.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, but the specific capacity dropped rapidly to 250.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 800 cycles, with a capacity decay rate of 0.062% per cycle. The excellent cycle performance of MPOC@RP stems from the fact that the abundant pores in OC provide sufficient buffer space for the volume expansion of RP, preventing RP from breaking and falling off from the electrode, and the strong capillary action inhibits the loss of intermediate products. However, the sheet structure of MXene does not have this function. When cycling tests were performed at a higher current density of 5 C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). In the first 300 cycles, the MPOC@RP anode dropped from 1416.5 to 929.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and then the capacity was stable at 899.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles, while the OC@RP anode only retained a reversible capacity of 354.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, far less than half of the specific capacity of the MPOC@RP anode, reflecting the importance of the electronic continuity generated by MXene mortar in MPOC for high-rate cycling. Even at an ultra-high current density of 10 C, the MPOC@RP anode provided a high specific capacity of nearly 700 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and maintained an extremely low decay rate of 0.035% per cycle.</p>
            
            <p>In order to explore more potential of MPOC@RP anode, its electrochemical performance in SIBs was further studied. The broad peak in the range of 0.2–0.5&nbsp;V in the CV curve corresponded to the sodiation process of RP through Na<sub>x</sub>P until the formation of Na<sub>3</sub>P.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0029_R_d62086571e927" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Small">29</a>, <a href="#smll202309702-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0042_R_d62086571e927" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - ACS Nano">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The oxidation peak at 0.73&nbsp;V corresponded to the desodiation process of Na<sub>x</sub>P to RP<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0041_R_d62086571e935" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ACS Nano">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17a</a>, Supporting Information). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17b</a> (Supporting Information) is the galvanostatic charge-discharge curves of the MPOC@RP anode at 0.1 C. The discharge and charge capacities of MPOC@RP were 3354.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 2417.5&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, and the ICE was 72.1%. The rate performance and stable cycle performance of the three electrodes are compared in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0003">3h,i</a>. Obviously, the MPOC@RP anode had the best rate performance among SIBs, with discharge capacities of 2408.1, 2195.5, 1918.6, 1584.1, 1131.2, and 819.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.3, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 C, respectively. When the current density returned to 0.1 C, the capacity quickly returned to 2142.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. When the specific capacity values were calculated based on the total mass of composite materials, MPOC@RP still exhibited a much higher capacities than conventional hard carbon (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). Meanwhile, the rate performance of the MPOC@RP anode also exceeds that of most phosphorus-based anodes in sodium-ion batteries reported in the literature,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0048_R_d62086571e961" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Adv. Energy Mater.">48</a>-<a href="#smll202309702-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0056_R_d62086571e961" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Nano Lett.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> (Supporting Information). Likewise, the MPOC@RP anode also exhibited good long-term cycle stability. After 1200 cycles at 2 C, the discharge capacity maintained at 628.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with the decay rate per cycle of 0.036%, and the Coulombic efficiency was close to 100%. In contrast, the OC@RP anode had a reversible specific capacity of 388.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1200 cycles, while the MXene@RP anode only delivered a low specific capacity of 117.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 400 cycles. The excellent electrochemical performance of the MPOC@RP anode in SIBs indicates that the wall-like microstructure not only promotes the transport of lithium ions, but also accelerates the migration of sodium ions to improve the capacity and rate performance and withstands the huge volume change caused by the sodiation during the cycle. By comparing with other red phosphorus-based anodes tested in lithium-ion batteries and sodium-ion batteries simultaneously (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0042_R_d62086571e980" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - ACS Nano">42</a>, <a href="#smll202309702-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0046_R_d62086571e980" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Chem">46</a>, <a href="#smll202309702-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0050_R_d62086571e980" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Adv. Mater.">50</a>, <a href="#smll202309702-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0057_R_d62086571e980" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Nano Lett.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> MPOC@RP anode exhibits significant advantages, indicating a great potential both in lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202309702-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202309702-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrochemical Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>The ion diffusion coefficient affected by factors such as electrode porosity, specific surface area, and electronic conductivity reflects the ability of ion migration in the battery and has a very important impact on the performance of the battery. Diffusion coefficient calculated according to Fick's second law formula is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202309702-fig-0004">4a,c</a>. During the lithiation/delithiation and sodiation/desodiation processes, the <i>D</i><sub>Li</sub> of the MPOC@RP anode was in the range of 10<sup>−12.5</sup>–10<sup>−10.5</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, and the <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> was in the range of 10<sup>−12.8</sup>–10<sup>−10.7</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> range. Compared with the MXene@RP anode, the MPOC@RP anode had a much higher of Li/Na ion diffusion coefficient throughout the discharge/charge process, indicating that the interconnected porous structure of OC facilitates the rapid transport of ions. What is noteworthy, MOC has the same composite but different nanostructure with MPOC, but it's battery properties no matter in specific capacity or cycle life were worse than MPOC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20a–c</a>, Supporting Information), which due to MXene nanosheets covering on the surface of OC made adverse to lithium ion diffusion into OC, which was inferred and proved by the lower ion diffusion coefficient of MOC compared with MPOC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20d</a>, Supporting Information). The compared results of electrochemical performance between MPOC and MOC demonstrates the significance of the ordered alternating assembly of MXene and OC to the battery property.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202309702-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a00b2734-46d0-4d6c-806e-eb177952760c/smll202309702-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a00b2734-46d0-4d6c-806e-eb177952760c/smll202309702-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/eedea0de-1154-4e83-8ac9-6cc7f5863d23/smll202309702-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a00b2734-46d0-4d6c-806e-eb177952760c/smll202309702-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202309702-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202309702" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Li<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion coefficient and b) overpotential values of MPOC@RP and MXene@RP anodes during lithiation and delithiation process. c) Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion coefficient and d) overpotential values of the two anodes during sodiation and desodiation process.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The enhancement of the ion diffusion coefficient promotes the alloy reaction kinetics, which is crucial to enhance the high-rate performance of the phosphorus anode. The resulting potential drop between the end of the relaxation step and the start of the current step, i. e., the overpotential, is used to assess the transient reaction kinetics of the electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309702-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309702-bib-0046_R_d62086571e1057" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Chem">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0004">4b,d</a>, whether in LIBs or SIBs, the MPOC@RP anode exhibited lower overpotential, indicating that the electrode possesses better reaction kinetics due to higher electron and ion concentrations at the electrochemical reaction interface. In addition, the MPOC@RP anode exhibited more stable reaction kinetics than the MXene@RP anode during cycling in LIBs and SIBs, demonstrating that the uniform distribution of electrons and ions promotes a stable electrochemical reaction in the electrode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>By testing the electrochemical impedance inside the battery and analyzing the electrochemical transfer resistance, the ability of the electrochemical reaction is effectively evaluated. The electrochemical impedance spectra of these electrodes are shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22 and S23</a> (Supporting Information). The MPOC@RP exhibited the smallest charge transfer resistance R<sub>ct</sub> among these anodes, since the MXene nanosheets provide a continuously conductive path for electrons and interconnectedly porous OC supports a rapid ion diffusion, improving the internal charge density at the reaction interface, increasing the charge transfer kinetics, and reducing the charge transfer resistance.</p>
            
            <p>To investigate the effect of nitrogen doping in MPOC on the battery performance, first-principles calculations based on density functional theory (DFT) was performed. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0005">5</a> lists the optimized structural models of different Li-P compounds after stable adsorption on N-doped porous carbon. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a> (Supporting Information), the binding energies of LiP<sub>7</sub>, LiP<sub>5</sub>, LiP, and Li<sub>3</sub>P on the carbon surface were 2.31, 2.11, 2.54, and 2.74&nbsp;eV, respectively, indicating that the carbon layer doped with N has a strong chemical adsorption on various soluble lithium polyphosphides. The above calculation results prove that the N-enhanced chemical adsorption also effectively anchor the intermediate phase during the charge–discharge process, inhibiting the loss of active materials and improving battery cycle performance.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202309702-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e6c3159c-5007-403b-a9d4-69054fcf9340/smll202309702-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e6c3159c-5007-403b-a9d4-69054fcf9340/smll202309702-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/cdf1f851-f618-4be4-ab91-930f91496bda/smll202309702-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e6c3159c-5007-403b-a9d4-69054fcf9340/smll202309702-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202309702-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202309702" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The optimized structures and adsorption energies of a) LiP<sub>7</sub>, b) LiP<sub>5</sub>, c) LiP, and d) Li<sub>3</sub>P on the N-doped graphene surface. Here, blue, red, yellow, and green spheres represent lithium, phosphorus, carbon, and nitrogen atoms, respectively.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>After the above characterization and analysis, it can be concluded that the excellent electrochemical performance of MPOC@RP is attributed to its reasonable microstructure design. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309702-fig-0006">6</a> shows the advantages of MPOC. MXene nanosheets act as a mortar to tightly connect OC units, improving the long-range order of the material structure and the continuity of electron transport. The interconnected pores of OC provide the host with a large specific surface area, shorten the diffusion distance of ions, and accelerate the ion diffusion transport. Both increase of electron and ion diffusion speed significantly enhances the battery reaction kinetics and improves the battery rate performance. N doping in OC provides strong chemical adsorption, effectively inhibits the loss of active materials, and increases the cycle life of the battery. The uniform distribution of active materials, electrons and ions promotes uniform electrochemical reactions and further increases the cycle stability of the battery.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202309702-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4318b493-cf6f-4c59-868d-6e3fe1bcbc4c/smll202309702-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4318b493-cf6f-4c59-868d-6e3fe1bcbc4c/smll202309702-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9ae4f1f0-dbdf-478c-8b28-50e4d26da021/smll202309702-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4318b493-cf6f-4c59-868d-6e3fe1bcbc4c/smll202309702-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202309702-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202309702" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of the lithiation/sodiation and subsequent delithiation/desodiation process of RP confined in the pores of MPOC for LIBs and SIBs.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202309702-sec-0060">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202309702-sec-0060-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In this work, a unique wall-like porous material was designed by a simple electrostatic separation-adsorption method, with MXene nanosheets as mortar and ordered porous carbon as bricks. Through the ordered alternating self-assembly of the two, it not only solved the easy stacking and low specific surface area of MXene nanosheets but also greatly enhanced the transfer rates of electrons and lithium ions simultaneously. When use RP as the active material, MPOC@RP anode exhibited a high-capacity output and stable long cycle life, much higher than that of MOC@RP, OC@RP, and MXene@RP anodes. The ion diffusion rate in MPOC@RP was two orders of magnitude higher than that of MXene@RP, and the charge transfer resistance was only one-third and one-sixth of OC@RP and MXene@RP, respectively. The first-principal calculation results show that N-doping in MPOC significantly improved the adsorption of the intermediate phase during the reaction process and extended the cycle life. The better performance of MPOC than MOC also proved the significance of the ordered nanostructure to the battery property. The fabrication of MPOC provides new design ideas for the construction of porous hosts for active materials such as silicon, phosphorus, sulfur, and selenium, but also offers a universal design strategy for other composites materials like MXene and carbon nanotubes, MXene and Graphene, and so on.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202309702-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202309702-sec-0070-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202309702-sec-0080">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202309702-sec-0090">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202309702-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202309702-sec-0100-title"> Preparation of OC</h4>
                  
                  <p>OC was synthesized by using silica opal as a hard template. 16.2&nbsp;g of ammonia water, 26&nbsp;g of ethanol, and 49.5&nbsp;g of water were added into a beaker, which was subsequently placed in a water bath at 28&nbsp;°C and stirred for 30&nbsp;min to obtain solution A. 44.7&nbsp;g ethyl orthosilicate was dissolved in 152&nbsp;g ethanol to form solution B. Solution B was quickly poured into solution A and continued stirring for 10&nbsp;h. The precipitate was centrifuged and washed three times with water and dispersed in deionized water to obtain a silica dispersion. The silica microsphere dispersion (10&nbsp;wt%) was distributed into 15&nbsp;mL vials. Silica spheres were assembled on the vial walls by a vertical drying method at 50&nbsp;°C to obtain ordered silica templates. 3&nbsp;g of Zn(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O and 6.6&nbsp;g of 2-methylimidazole were dissolved in methanol solution (60&nbsp;mL) and N, N-dimethylformamide (180&nbsp;mL) solution respectively, and quickly mixed and stirred at room temperature for 3&nbsp;h to obtain the precursor solution. Then it was infiltrated into a silica template overnight and after heated at 90&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h. ZIF-8 filled in the gaps of the silica template was obtained (denoted as ZIF-8@SiO<sub>2</sub>). Then the carbonization of ZIF-8@SiO<sub>2</sub> was carried out at 900&nbsp;°C for 5&nbsp;h in a tube furnace in Ar flow. Then the silica template was etched away by hydrofluoric acid (HF) solution to obtain N-doped ordered porous carbon (OC).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202309702-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202309702-sec-0110-title"> Preparation of MXene (Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub>)</h4>
                  
                  <p>Single-layer or few-layer MXene nanosheets were prepared by selectively etching the Al layer on MAX-Ti<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub>. 2&nbsp;g lithium fluoride (LiF) was dissolved in 40&nbsp;mL 9.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> Hydrochloric acid (HCl) aqueous solution. 2&nbsp;g MAX precursor phase was slowly added to the above mixed solution in batches and stirred at 35&nbsp;°C for 24&nbsp;h. The solid precipitate was centrifuged and washed repeatedly with deionized water until the pH of the supernatant reached 6. Finally, the precipitate was ultrasonically stripped under bubbling argon flow and centrifuged at 3500&nbsp;rpm to obtain a uniform dark supernatant containing Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>X</sub> nanosheets. 2D MXene flakes were obtained after vacuum freeze-drying for 24&nbsp;h.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202309702-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202309702-sec-0120-title"> Preparation of MPOC</h4>
                  
                  <p>2.149&nbsp;g PDDA (35% solution), 0.363&nbsp;g tris(hydroxymethyl)aminomethane, 0.173&nbsp;g NaCl, and 150&nbsp;mL H<sub>2</sub>O were added to the vial, and a mixed aqueous solution was formed after ultrasonication for 5&nbsp;min. Subsequently, OC was dispersed in the mixed aqueous solution for 30&nbsp;min and washed three times with deionized water to remove the excess PDDA, and then freeze-dried for 24&nbsp;h. Herein, PDDA has successfully functionalized OC to obtain a positively charged ordered porous carbon (POC). The MXene and POC aqueous solutions were mass-controllably dropped into the beaker at a ratio of 1:2, respectively. The mixed solution was sonicated for 1&nbsp;h and stirred for 12&nbsp;h, centrifuged with deionized water, and then freeze-dried for 24&nbsp;h to obtain the MPOC. For comparison, MOC was prepared by mechanically mixing the MXene nanosheets and OC.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202309702-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202309702-sec-0130-title"> Preparation of MPOC@RP</h4>
                  
                  <p>The MPOC and red phosphorus were mixed in a weight ratio of 1:2 and vacuum-sealed in a small quartz tube. The mixture was heated at 550&nbsp;°C for 3&nbsp;h first. Subsequently, it was cooled to 260&nbsp;°C with a cooling rate of 1 °C min<sup>−1</sup> and kept for 24&nbsp;h in a tube furnace. In this period, the condensed white P was transformed to red P. After the quartz tube was naturally cooled to room temperature, it was quickly moved to an argon-filled glove box and opened. The prepared MPOC@RP composite was taken out, washed with carbon disulfide to remove the white P remaining during condensation, and then dried in a vacuum oven at 60&nbsp;°C for 8&nbsp;h. Under the same preparation conditions, MOC@RP, OC@RP, and MXene@RP comparison samples were prepared.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202309702-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202309702-sec-0140-title"> The GITT Calculation</h4>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">The ion diffusion coefficient was calculated by the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT), and the speed of ion diffusion in the electrode was analyzed. The specific calculation formula is as follows:

                     <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202309702-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                           <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/a310c2fa-ab4c-4b6d-8ab7-573dc81f2529/smll202309702-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202309702:smll202309702-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/smll202309702-math-0001.png">
                              <semantics>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi>D</mi>
                                    <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                                    <mfrac>
                                       <mn>4</mn>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi>π</mi>
                                          <mi>τ</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </mfrac>
                                    <msup>
                                       <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                          <mfrac>
                                             <mrow>
                                                <msub>
                                                   <mi>m</mi>
                                                   <mi>B</mi>
                                                </msub>
                                                <msub>
                                                   <mi>V</mi>
                                                   <mi>M</mi>
                                                </msub>
                                             </mrow>
                                             <mrow>
                                                <msub>
                                                   <mi>M</mi>
                                                   <mi>B</mi>
                                                </msub>
                                                <mi>S</mi>
                                             </mrow>
                                          </mfrac>
                                       </mfenced>
                                       <mn>2</mn>
                                    </msup>
                                    <msup>
                                       <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                          <mfrac>
                                             <mrow>
                                                <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                                <msub>
                                                   <mi>E</mi>
                                                   <mi>s</mi>
                                                </msub>
                                             </mrow>
                                             <mrow>
                                                <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                                <msub>
                                                   <mi>E</mi>
                                                   <mi>τ</mi>
                                                </msub>
                                             </mrow>
                                          </mfrac>
                                       </mfenced>
                                       <mn>2</mn>
                                    </msup>
                                 </mrow>
                                 <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}D = \frac{4}{{\pi \tau }}{\left( {\frac{{{m}_B{V}_M}}{{{M}_BS}}} \right)}^2{\left( {\frac{{\Delta {E}_s}}{{\Delta {E}_\tau }}} \right)}^2\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                              </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
                  </div>
                  
                  <p>Among them, <i>D</i> is the ion diffusion coefficient; <i>τ</i> is the titration time; <i>m</i><sub>B</sub> is the mass of the active material; <i>V</i><sub>M</sub> and <i>M</i><sub>B</sub> are the molar volume and molar mass, respectively; <i>S</i> is the electrode surface area. Δ<i>E</i><sub>S</sub> is the quasi-thermodynamic equilibrium potential difference before and after the current pulse, and Δ<i>E</i><sub>τ</sub> is the potential difference during the current pulse (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202309702-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202309702-sec-0150-title"> First-Principles Calculations</h4>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">First-principles calculations were performed by density functional theory (DFT) to investigate the effect of nitrogen doping in MPOC on battery performance. Li-P compounds (LiP<sub>7</sub>, LiP<sub>5</sub>, LiP, Li<sub>3</sub>P) were selected as representative soluble polyphosphides. Considering that the active material was mainly encapsulated in the porous carbon crystal in MPOC, the graphite layer doped with N was selected as the substrate model for simulating the binding energy. For the calculation of Li-P compound adsorption energy, the following formula was used:

                     <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202309702-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                           <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/00870965-0601-4fa7-b07f-d2d498e7f6f1/smll202309702-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202309702:smll202309702-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/smll202309702-math-0002.png">
                              <semantics>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>
                                       <mi>b</mi>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi>a</mi>
                                          <mi>d</mi>
                                          <mi>s</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi>N</mi>
                                          <mo>−</mo>
                                          <mi>C</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi>L</mi>
                                          <mi>i</mi>
                                          <mo>−</mo>
                                          <mi>P</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </msub>
                                 </mrow>
                                 <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{E}_b = {E}_{ads} - {E}_{N - C} - {E}_{Li - P}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                              </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
                  </div>
                  
                  <p>Among them, <i>E</i><sub>N-C</sub> and <i>E</i><sub>Li-P</sub> are the energies of the single porous carbon matrix and Li-P compound, respectively, and <i>E</i><sub>ads</sub> is the total energy of adsorbed polyphosphides on the carbon surface.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202309702-sec-0160">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202309702-sec-0160-title"> Material Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>The phase analysis was characterized by X-Ray Diffraction (XRD) (Germany, D8 ADVANCE Da Vinci with Cu Kα radiation) operated at 40 KV and 30&nbsp;mA and by Raman Microscope (Senterra R200-L/Senterra R200-L). Autosorb-IQ3 probed the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption measurement. The morphology analysis was examined by Field Emission Scanning Electron Microscope (FESEM) (Czech Republic, MIRA3-LHM) equipped with EDX (XFlash6160) and by Field Emission Transmission Electron Microscope (FETEM) (FEI Talos F200X).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202309702-sec-0170">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202309702-sec-0170-title"> Electrochemical Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>The electrochemical performance was tested by the coin cell that assembled in an argon-filled glovebox. The working electrodes were fabricated by mixing composites, super C45, and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) binder with a mass ratio of 70:15:15, and <i>N</i>-methyl pyrrolidone (NMP) was used as the solvent to form a homogeneous slurry, which was painted on a Cu foil and dried in a vacuum oven overnight at 60&nbsp;°C. The LIBs were fabricated with lithium metal as the counter electrode, Celgard 2400 membrane as the separator, and 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> LiPF6 in a mixture of ethylene carbonate (EC)/diethyl carbonate (DEC) (1:1 by volume) with an addition of 5&nbsp;wt% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) as the electrolyte. The SIBs were constructed using the sodium sheet (homemade) as the counter electrode, glass fiber membrane (Whatman, GF/D) was selected as the separator, and 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> salt in EC/dimethyl carbonate (DMC) (1:1) plus 5&nbsp;wt% FEC was used as the electrolyte. Battery property measurements were performed by the Neware battery test instrument (BTS-610) within the voltage range of 0.01–3.0&nbsp;V (versus Li<sup>+</sup>/Li) or 0.01–2.5&nbsp;V (versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). The mass loading of the red phosphorus in MPOC@RP, MXene@RP, OC@RP, and MOC@RP electrodes were ≈1.24, 1.06, 1.37, and 1.13&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, respectively. The CV and EIS measurements were performed via an electrochemical workstation (CHI760E). The scan rate of the CV test was 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, and the frequency range at the open-circuit potential on EIS measurement was 100&nbsp;kHz to 10 mHz.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
